RATING: 6
Critical to the game is the process of talking to enemies and allies. Secondary are the actual mechanics of moving armies and fleets (mastered quickly). Those with a keen understanding of human nature and are able to think and talk fast are rewarded rather than those with clever strategy. The game closely mirrors life with its undeserved failures and unexpected successes. Diplomacy is a great game.

Having said all that, the game does encourage "negative" behaviour - you *will* be forced on occasion to lie, use coercion or threats, be forced to trust an opponent known to be lying, etc. These aspects are all part of the rough and tumble of the game (which some love and others loathe). Finding 6 players is near impossible, so I rather recommend the excellent 2-player variant Intimate Diplomacy. ID removes the lying, back-stabbing and other negative aspects or 7-player Dip, but retains the rich game play. Its also takes less than half the time to play!

Diplomacy: 5
Intimate Diplomacy: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
The single worst game ever made.

As a "wargame," it is an epic fail: it takes place in the early 20th century and Austria-Hungary is as just as likely to be allies with Russia as they are with Germany.  Yeeeeahhh.... Not.

Combat-wise, it gets worse: I have 2 meeples and you have 1, so I win.  Yep, that's it.  Because never in history has a numerically inferior force won...ever.  Terrain advantages?  Russian winters?  Non-exsistant.  And it has a stacking limit of 1.  And get this, the quality and quantity of troops are entirely the same: Russian meeples are the same as German meeples.  Yeeeeahhh.... Not.

So how do you play?  You get together and discuss secret plans with the other players to have your meeples gang up on other meeples, because that 1 stacking limit makes it tough to conquer anybody by yourself.  Really?  Yeah, so the only way to win is to have 2 areas attack one area.  Somewhere along the way, you may have to lie to one of your allies, because you want to rule Europe.  That's pretty much it.

Now, why do I call these "meeples?"  Because this isn't a WARGAME!!!  It's the first Euro.  Consider what they have in common: cubes (at least the version I once had contained cubes), a lame-brain pasted-on theme that has nothing to do with reality but rather is a way to teach the "mechanics", and the most important common denominator: dreadfully dull game play.  Oh, and did I mention this "historical" wargame gives Russia a stronger army than Germany -- in the early 20th century?  BWAHAHAHAHAHAHAHA!!!  But if you don't do that, Russia will get knocked out early.  That, chilluns, is called a "balancing mechanism" which is prevalent in Euros.  Yeah, it has player elimination, but hey, this was the first Euro: it was pioneering the genre.  Even the Eurogamers knew this sucked and required improvements, so they kicked out the conflict and kept the rest.

This "game" came about when Satan pro-created with a Kansas Jayhawk and the two most evil forces in history combined all their evilness and put it in a box and thrust it on the world.  This "game" is akin to the videotape in the movie "The Ring" (or "Ringu" if you saw the original).

If you want a war-based diplomatic-style game, go play Origins.  If you want a game that involves back stabbing with interesting theme and more realistic game play, go play Battlestar Galactica.  If you want a WWI-game, go play something like Paths of Glory or Guns of August.  Heck, go play Castle Risk.  All of them beat this game.  If you can't find them, just give up the WWI idea, and go play something else.  If there are no other games possible, just give up gaming at that time and go do something more enjoyable, like pull off your fingernails with a pair of pliers.  

I imagine that the 7th level of Hell is a small room with no doors or windows, and the KU fight song is blaring over speakers that are built in the wall and protected.  A game of Diplomacy is set up on a table, and seated at that table are Justin Bieber, Pauley Shore, Fred Phelps, and Barney the Dinosaur, and they have patiently been waiting for the final player to join them, and now they can share eternal damnation with you.  ARRRGHH!!! It makes me puke just to think about it!

So there you have it: this game is so bad, that I wish I could give it a "0," but since I cannot, I went back and re-rated my other "1" games "1.1" when I don't do fractional ratings.  However, I wanted to be perfectly clear without any room for confusion or misinterpretation that this is worse than all of the others.

Is this game "broken?"  Yes.  It's broken because it breaks everything around it: it breaks your joy.  It breaks your innocence.  It breaks your will to live.  It's a broken game because games are [i]supposed[/i] to be fun.  If you stuck needles under my fingernails and called it a game, you may claim it's not broken, but I would beg to differ, and I would rather have you do that than subject me to Diplomacy.

I have a saying that dinosaurs can make any game better.  I am wrong.  I think Diplomacy brought the extinction of the dinosaurs: they died of boredom.

RATING: N/A
 
 
 
 
Diplomacy is a game best played with 7 players. Each player represents one of the Great Powers of Europe in the years just prior to the Great War, (World War I). England, Germany, Russia, Turkey, Austria-Hungary, Italy and France. Each is independent of the other. At the start of the game, the players draws to determine which Great Power each will represent. As soon as one Great power controls 18 Supply Centers, that player has "gained control of Europe". Tactically, this is a very simple game. Since it has two types of units; Armies and Fleets, each with the same basic abilities and limitations. Strategically this can be as complex as you wish to make it. Remember: you don't represent a soldier or sailor or general or admiral. Each player must be an imperial leader, a politican, an ambassador.

Expected playing time is about 4 - 5 hours.

RATING: 8
You better like negotiation. That seems obvious, but you will not have a good time otherwise, because that 95% of what the game is. Not only that, but it is basically impossible to win by yourself because of how deterministic everything is, so you need help from others and they need it from you. Alliances will naturally form, and finding the best way to bend those to your advantage (or eventually break them) is the crux of the game. I adore this, but if you find yourself on the wrong end of a broken promise it can ruin your game and you'll find yourself sitting around for a while. This game absolutely still holds up, but I think there are probably ways to modernize it a bit as well.

RATING: 9
Avalon Hill (Feb. 1982 / 2nd Edition) #819
Clearly the best 7-player strategy on the market.  This game is not for sensitive or weak gamers.  This game requires alliances to be made and broken throughout the game. The mechanics of the game are very simple (only 2 types of game pieces - armies and fleets) but the skill required to excel game after game is daunting.  Highly recommended.  Once you buy and learn the game, then the game becomes truly international in scope and will allow you to interact (via Internet; not included in the boxed set!) with die hard Diplomacy players from around the globe.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Love it or hate it?  One of the most divisive games out there.  With no luck (except for which country you draw), this game relies entirely on your diplomacy skills.  It's extremely intense, and it's hard not to take betrayals personally.  It's best to play repeatedly with the same group.  If you just play once, there's a large incentive to screw over other people.  By playing repeatedly with the same group, that incentive is reduced -- keep screwing over people, and everyone will gang up on you.

Also, probably best not to play this game with family members or significant others (if you want them to stay family...or significant).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Classic war-themed game of treachery and alliances. Play one of seven countries during WWI and beat, cheat, and cooperate with opponents to gain 18 centers to win, using your armies and fleets through hidden orders.

RATING: 10
This might be the best game ever made.  :-P

Perhaps I am merely lucky to have friends who know to keep the game in the game.  

Also, my most favorable games have been play-by-email with people I see on a regular basis in real life.  This gives it an interesting tension to the decision to stab, since I DON'T have the luxury of anonymity, but still get all day to plan, scheme, and conspire with everyone in the game.  Interestingly enough, this gives the game all the emotional impact of face to face combined with the chance to plan optimal moves.  In my opinion, it's the only way to play it.

And played that way, it is the nail-biting game to end all nail-biting games.  This is elegance and tension in board game form.

RATING: 10
I admit this game has many flaws. It takes a long time. It includes player elimination, and I expect 1-2 players to be eliminated in the first three hours. It plays best with seven. And it's really only good for certain types of people. But it is a pure implementation of the negotiation/political mechanic. Yes, there are rules about the fleets and armies on the board, but they are almost irrelevant, merely venues for adjudicating the real game, which is in every player's head. And it's brilliant.

RATING: N/A
Got a second copy for $2 from local Op-Shop (Cat Protection). It is unplayed (pieces still joined together) and I've got to assume it's the 1982 edition as it has a 1982 Avalon Hill catalogue in the box.
In fact my original copy was a sale copy bought in the eighties from Angus & Robertson. Reason for price reduction was 'missing pieces' but there had been an additional full set of pieces added to the slightly deficient set, so I actually have double complements for most nations (16 armies, 16 fleets) with 12 for Turkish and French fleets and English armies, with the French having to settle for the original complement of only 8 armies!

RATING: 3.4
I really detest Diplomacy. I used to like it, back when I was younger and didn't care so much how I treated others. This game is absolutely and only about lying to other players. Eventually, you will have to do it at some point in the game. You will manipulate and lie, and you will be purposefully manipulated and decieved. If that floats your boat, this game is for you. I find it potentially destructive of relationships, meaning the small group of gamers can become smaller because of the playing of a game. That can already happen because of any number of other misbehaviors that may occur in gaming. No reason to put a game on the table in which relationship destruction is the main tactical tool.

RATING: 7
I have yet to find a game with more strategy and less luck.  I have only played two games with a full seven players, and that was only accomplished via email.  The game format lends itself very well to email since all orders are executed simultaneously.  A face-to-face game is supposed to last about six hours.  Our email games have lasted about six months (playing one turn per week).  This game practically consumes my thoughts when playing by email since there are so many options to consider to the point that my wife is annoyed because I only talk about Diplomacy.

Very involved all the time.  For more info www.diplom.org is by far the best source I have found for Diplomacy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
YES!  This is definitely one of my all time favorites.  Yes, it does take a long time.  Yes, a full compliment of opponents makes it ideal.  Yes, you can be eliminated and sit out for many more hours.  That all comes with the package, but what a package it is!  Aside from the starting empires, there is absolutely NO randomness.  All successes and failures hinge on your ability to cajole, lie to , and/or brainwash your opponents into seeing things your way.  You HAVE to walk into this game knowing you will be stabbed in the back, and probably by your best friend.  It's just part of the game, and you have to deal with it.  Are you wily enough to sense when that will happen and have a nasty trap laid for the unscrupulous turncoat on the right turn?  Or will you show your hand too soon, creating distrust in the most innocent suggestion?  Get big too quick, and everyone wants to knock off the big guy.  But don't grow fast enough, and the buzzards start to circle.  Classic!  If you want everybody to be friends and end with a 7-way tie, this is NOT the game for you.  But, if you wish to test your diplomatic skills, knowing that your friends may hate you for years to come, then this is the game for you!  [Market price]

RATING: N/A
Good news...bad news.  
The bad news is that this copy looks like it has taken a hard fall on the upper right hand corner:  Bottom box corner is split and wrinkled; corner of lid is nearly split and wrinkled down the side for approx. 3 inches; the corners of the conference maps are wrinkled; and if you look close, you can spy some minor wrinkling on the corner of the board.
The good news is that this game is unplayed.  Conference maps are still attached as a pad, all four clips to hold map sections together are present, as is the original rule set.  Finally, all the pieces (WOODEN) are present and accounted for...still in factory sealed bag.  Shoot, even if you're not a big Diplomacy fan, those 112 wooden playing pieces are quite a draw.  I almost kept this set for myself, as I learned the game with the wooden pieces, but maybe someone out there would appreciate the game more.
Traded away at GameStorm16 math trade for Justinian.

RATING: N/A
Wonderful condition of this classic.  WOOD PIECES!  Upon counting, this set has the correct number of pieces (112), however, there is an odd count (intentional?).  All countries have 8 army blocks, and all but two have 8 fleet blocks.  Turkey (yellow) has 9 navy blocks, while France (turquoise) only has 7.  As it looks as if this game was never played, or only played once, these seem to be the original blocks that came with the game.  Go figure.  Board is in great condition, and box shows minor shelf wear only on the corners.

RATING: 8
Not without reasons is Diplomacy the classic it has been for over than half a century: it has depth and interaction without being overly fiddly. It’s just a little difficult to grasp at first, as granting and negating supports is not always as intuitive as it looks.

Alliances, betrayals (both intended and unintended), rivalries and paybacks just add some spices to the fun, as they make interaction the core mechanism of this game (which, being it titled «Diplomacy», is to be expected): sadly though, since it is the key element, interaction also drags the game and stretches it overly too long.

RATING: 5
This is a great, classic game.  So why the average rating? Two main reasons...

Firstly, this game suffers immensely from Analysis Paralysis... i.e. players that take F-O-R-E-V-E-R on their turns. This is the reason I will not play this game.

Secondly, too many people get angry over the eventual, and pretty much required, betrayal they will face by the end of the game.  Alliances (i.e. Diplomacy) are often what help you win the game... but if you want to win the game as a single player, that means you WILL betray. This isn't a problem for my group, but it is for many.

RATING: 7
On a purely sentimental level, Diplomacy is probably my third most-loved game, after Axis and Allies and Euchre.

It's my primary link into the world of organized boardgaming. I'm associated with the Potomac Tea & Knife Society (http://www.ptks.org/), although I no longer live in the DC area. My favorite regular tournament is DixieCon, run by David Hood in Chapel Hill, NC. (http://www.dixiecon.com/) 

I strongly prefer face-to-face to email/judge play (e-communication is too low context for me to enjoy.) If played online, I prefer gunboat (no talking). 

Both the simultaneous movement and non-binding negotiation aspects of Diplomacy appeal to me. I'm a "Survivor"-contestant-wannabe, and those two aspects of Diplomacy present strong parallels to what attracts me to the game of "Survivor".

Rough country preference: FERTGIA

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is one of the best games I own or have ever played. If you like highly social, strategy games, give this one a shot.

Pros:
-No dice! It's like Risk (based on world domination), but you aren't left to the mercy of multiple dice rolls.
-It is very social. In order to last even a couple rounds you must form alliances. This is the games major appeal for me - everyone is interested in forming alliances.
-You have to break alliances eventually. This is why the game doesn't get boring - anyone can turn on you at any time. How long do you trust your allies?
-Moves are made simultaneously. I like this mechanic - everyone writes down their rules, so there is no waiting for your turn; it's always your turn.

Cons:
-Time. I have managed to organize about five games of diplomacy in the many years I have had it. Unless you have a dedicated group of game players with enough time on their hands, it is difficult to find people to play this game.
-Difficult rules. It takes a long time to teach the game to new players.
-Move resolution is a lengthy process. It can take a while to resolve everyone written moves. If the players aren't competitive, they will quickly lost interest in the part of the game.

The pros heavily outweigh the cons for this game, if you have the time and right group of players.

RATING: 6
Hard to rate this one on the BGG scale. Definitely a classic worth 10 on many fronts, a near perfect rendition of simplicty and elegance, it is not a game so much as a phenomenon! Yet I can'trae it 10 which says: "Always want to play and expect this will never change". That's just not true. 

The reality is it's a long game, hard to play well, highly entertaining but also very draining at times and emotive with treachery at its very core. It suffers the dropout phenomenon, as in you can be eliminated and have nothig to do, or just as bad all but eliminated and an army to push around. 

So while it is nearing perfection as a fantastic game, it's also a real chore to play and doesn't find much place in my life. So I can't give it more than the 6 which sates "will play sporadically if in the right mood".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Juego de negociación pura. El escenario es europa con 7 potencias (francia, uk, italia, alemania, austria, turquia y rusia) en primavera de 1901. Hay dos turnos por año.. primavera y otoño y cada uno tiene una fase de negociación, otra de establecer ordenes (se escriben en un papel ocultas) y luego ejecución.  No hay dados ni azar en absoluto. Todo depende de cómo seas capaz de negociar con el resto de los jugadores y de los compromisos a los que llegues. 
Al final de cada año, hay un recuento de puntos de producción y un ajuste de las unidades. No hay combates ni bajas (normalmente). Me gusta porque además incluye un número muy reducido de fichas..

Altamente recomendable para 7 jugadores. Yo, además de la vez que acabo de registrar en la bgg, la jugué hace tiempo en otras tres o cuatro ocasiones y siempre triunfó. Influye en gran medida el talante de cada jugador. 

Recomendable: Evitar perfiles tipo sergio.

RATING: N/A
*  $12
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
RULES: ***** - The rules of the game strike me as very clear and easy to comprehend. The artwork in the rulebook may seem a little bland, however, it is effective at explaining the main concepts and it's not difficult to understand the mechanics of the game.
STRATEGY: ***** - The planning aspect in this game is huge. Every country has a lot of intermediate objectives to choose from, and choosing the right allies for these objectives are crucial. Careful planning is very rewarding in this game.
TACTICS: **** - The tactical choices may be a little bit restricted, because the units do not offer much variance in the game, however, every once in a while situations arise, where a wise support or attack may catch your opponent unexpectedly.
LUCK: ***** - There is absolutely no luck in any form of randomizer, which I find an awesome feature as I prefer to be outwitted by my opponent and not by my lousy dice rolling (which is seriously lousy) or tile drawing. Of course, you may not always outguess your opponent, so there is definitely some luck in the game, but at least it's controllable.
PLAYER INTERACTION: ***** - Player interaction here IS the game, its body and its soul. Order resolution and battles on the table are just a minor addition to the great psychological wars that roam during negotiation. CAUTION!!! Please do not forget, that Diplomacy is just a game. Treachery in the negotiations is but a part of it. If you take it personal, with all due respect, you're an immature prick.
REPLAYABILITY: **** - The games do have some repeating patterns in them because it is quite a bit analyzed all over the Internet, however, litterally thousands of variants of this game will keep it fresh for you even if the original game will lose its taste for you.
THEME: *** - The battle system is quite a bit unrealistic and usually provides no deaths in the battlefields (most of the armies diperse because of lack of supplies), however, on strategic level it simulates linear warfare quite good, and the diplomatic aspect of the game is simply great. Overall it does feel a bit too abstract.
BALANCE: *** - Since the map is not symetric, some countries are stronger than others. Personally I find Italy and Turkey very dull and Austria is simply a cannon fodder. Despite that, I have seen all of the countries play successfully, it's just that some of them require quite a bit more effort than others.
DOWNTIME: **** - You have a lot to discuss during the negotiation phase. Sometimes you have so much to cover, you run like mad from person to person. Order resolution may be duller though, because it's just a lot of mechanical work pushing armies and fleets around, however, it's still usually fun to watch how everything folds out, so it's bearable.
ACCESSIBILITY: ** - This game is insanely long and requires insanely many players. Quite frankly, you almost don't have a chance to play it as a board game, however, this game is more than suited to be played as PbeM. Actually, I find it at least ten times more fun that way. A turn a day instead of a turn every 30 minutes will make it quite a bit easier to find seven players.

RATING: 10
I think to be a true gamer (not something everyone who enjoys playing games should or needs to aspire to), one has to master the play of Diplomacy, and should at least win from time to time. It's sort of a trial by fire that everyone who aspires to grasp the Zen of gaming should pass through and master. It teaches you (sometimes brutally) all the skills you need to be a good social gamer: the ability to both play quite competitively and still walk away from it at the end of the day as just a game, realizing that winning isn't that important; the ability to assume a role in a game that is not you, and appreciate what's happening in the game as important and interesting but apart from you as a real person; and the need for constructive social interaction even within the confines of what is a cutthroat game, and that being a jerk is no more productive here than in any other reality. And of course Diplomacy is a masterful game in and of itself, with simple, easily-grasped rules that enable a tremendously rich game experience. I particularly like how Diplomacy games evolve an interesting narrative and do so organically, without the "plot coercion" common in modern games (especially the card-driven games like Here I Stand or The Napoleonic Wars, even with all their strengths).
---
A true classic which everyone should play ... once, at least.  Must admit to being a little surprised about the length compaints; I once played 3 games in one day (not somthing I'd recommend), and games took about 6 hours - long enough to be a problem, admittedly, but still faster than many American games (Junta is longer, and has a fraction of the content). Still, it hardly needs to be stated that the nature of the game will change dramatically with the players, and players who can't separate games from reality will not be desirable to play with. Still, many years ago, this was the staple of my gaming diet. Rather long by today's standards, has a rather sever player-elimination problem, can be arbitrary and a bit vindictive for the kinder, gentler gaming generation; but for a negotiation game with an international power-politics feel, it can't be beat.
---
Edition Notes: Basically, to get a good copy of this game, you need to go back to the old Avalon Hill bookcase edition with the wooden pieces. Most other editions of this game have been crap. The plastic pieces (stars and anchors) that Avalon Hill introduced in some editions were strange, and were so small they made the board look vacant. The "new" AH edition, that used the Monopoly cannons and ships for pieces, was actually fairly nice, but the gargantuan box was a bit of a downer. I kinda liked the Gibson Games edition with garish colors and the bullets for pieces, but more for its brazenly tacky color choices than anything reasonable. The new WotC edition has a nice price point, but the pieces are not great; at least it's a small-ish box!

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is my favourite board game of all time. The complex strategy aspect (while still easy to learn), the secretive deal making (and deal breaking!), the nerve-wrecking order resolution phase where things fall apart before you're eyes, and the sweet revenge of a well-played stab in the back - these are all things that make this game very awesome.

On the downside is the fact that it takes at least 6 hours to play, and that's if everyone is experienced. And it can easily take longer, especially if you play all the way to the end (which you don't have to). While I don't mind playing long board games, most of my friends do, and so it's really difficult to get people together. And the game really only works if you have all seven powers represented. So you need seven players - no more, no less. But when you do manage to get the time and the people together it's one of the best board game experiences you can have.

It's also great to play online asynch, either via e-mail or through a website.

RATING: 8
In the beginning, there were wargames, RPG and Diplomacy.  Dip stands in a class of it's own.  Fully deserves the title it shares with real diplomacy, "The Greatest Game." RATING: 10
It isn't merely a game, but a genre - and for some, such as me, a hobby. In elegance and sophistication it's right up there next to Chess. And a whole lot more enjoyable and satisfying - though Chess afficionados are bound to disagree.

There are literally hundreds of variants. The Standard map is excellent, but I'll argue that the 1900 variant surpasses it. Anyone into Diplomacy, owes himself checking out 1900.

That said, Diplomacy isn't for everyone. (But then, Chess isn't either). For the full experience, you have to have the right sort of players.

Further, this game works brilliantly per email. Indeed, I argue it is thus "purer". Face-to-Face and PBEM are quite different and both great.

RATING: 9.3
Diplo is a powerful game.  It lives in a particular space of social interaction that few other pastimes reach; it has the innate ability to destroy friendships.  The incredible thing about Diplo is that it takes two opposite ideas- co-operative play and ruthless, cut-throat competition - and combines them in a simple, abstracted ruleset.  Is it an enjoyable gameplay experience?  I don't know.  Diplomacy sessions seem to transcend being just a game and becomes an Event.  You look forward to playing it with an eerie mix of dread and wonderment.  So while the game itself is excellent and rates perhaps an 8, it deserves much respect for the environment it creates around the table.  It could be argued that there is no greater political simulator.

RATING: 10
For me: The best game ever!
Even though: it is so hard to find the people to play it, it is unbalanced, it is unfair, it is mean ... oh - I love this game!!!!

And for me, THE game, that was the gateway to boardgaming. I had my first game being eight years old (back in 1977 - oh my!) ... Visiting my Sister in New York, we actually played it with also some professional diplomats ... it was an amazing expirence for me - even though I drew Austria-Hungary ... and ended up with only one army in Trieste. Of course all the adults where feeling quite bad for having me beaten up so bad - and offered all kinds of pacts and support - but I decided to move my army in the adriatic sea - rather commiting suicide, than to survive only upon the mercy of others. But deep inside, I swore to play better in the future! 

Great, great game - where we had the most amazing roleplaying going on around this game, with spying, hiding under the table to burn up the negotiation-map during the negotiations of the opposed alliance ... so everybody had fun. but also I had several quite serious rounds, where the hard negotiations went on and on ... great, great game ... the best game I know - so simple rules, so much depth - and so much open space for players to create their kind of game, while playing it.

RATING: 6.5
Flawed but beautiful. Just such clean lines.

We've found the best way to play is to get exactly 15 people together. 2 per country (3 is too many because one player will sit out, and if you're alone it's easy to get offended) makes 14, and 1 dedicated referee to read orders and fulfill them. At that scale, you're always going to have new players, and it's important to have the ref read over the first few seasons of orders to avoid feel-bads when people submit impossible moves.

Online is a completely different game and totally absorbing, but it's too much for me these days.

RATING: 7
Diplomacy is certainly a seminal game for wargamers.  the concept that one can have a wargame without any randomness (no dice, no paper scissors stone) is a great one, and for that I appluad the makers.  My reservations about Diplomacy are that the rules are hoorendously conplex and quite innaccesable for a new group of player to get to grips with.  Some rules even seem self-contradictory when one reads them for the first time.  The game take a VERY long time too, which puts a lot of people off; lots of friendships can be put to the test during a game!  

Getting over all of these difficulties will leave you with a highly engaging game though, one that all serious gamers should take time to try.

RATING: 3
Some don't like this game because of the possible (and likely) treachery aspects. I don't mind this "issue" - it's an vital part of the game and adds to the tension when playing this sort of "negotiation"-games.

My grief is a different: The game takes so loooong! And, while a long play time might not be a downside in itself, it is very likely that some players will be eliminated in the course of the game.

The combination of both: expanded playtime and elimination is, what distracts me from this game! It is almost unplayable in private gaming groupes, as sooner or later one player will be excluded.

("Diplomacy" might be interesting to play on a gaming convention, when you may easily find another group to have fun. But then, it simply might last too long for this occasion, too...)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.8
One of the best games of all time.  Pure genius.  No crazy dice rolls.  No phone book thick manuals.  Armies are equal in strength and the only way to win is to convince some sucker to help you.  What a victory in gaming.  One downside to this game is that it really only works well with a total of 7 players.  And in addition, it also suffers the same fault of all FFA (free for all) games; players give up.  Even if they are not losing, once they "believe" someone has the advantage they tend to stop trying.  So good games suddenly end.  Good team games help prevent this!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Best game ever!  Pure-strategy and negotiation, no dice or cards.  The only problems I have with this game are....  You need exactly 7 players who can be dedicated to the game for the entire day.  Players must leave the game as a game and not bring in outside influences or hold grudges after the game.  My experience is that most people cannot handle it when someone lies to them and will throw tempers and/or quit the game, which then throws off the balance of the game.  If you have any newbies in the game, they are always getting their written orders wrong which really messes up the game.  I am generally willing to act as game master (an 8th player) to run the game, collecting and validating orders, providing simple help to newbies early in the game, and acting as time referee.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
The ultimate friendship destroyer!
This game is a classic.  Easy to learn, a lifetime to master.  There is a fantastic community of gamers around this game.  I used to play tournament and Play By Mail Diplomacy back in the day and I have the mental scars to prove it.
Diplomacy is very much like Chess, but between 7 players.  It has its opening moves, counters, etc, and the character of the game unfurls, but with a non-binding 15 minute diplomacy period between each turn which is where the real action happens.  It is a lesson in trust, learning how to read people, detecting lies, and, ultimately, how to accept defeat and betrayal, which makes the winning games even sweeter.
Having a thick skin is mandatory.  Holding grudges is something best left at the door.  
Trust no-one!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Strip off all the chrome of a multiplayer, wargame and you have this.  Excellent game that I've only been able to play twice (once was via email).  It all comes down to your ability to negotiate and convince others to attack someone else.  This is really the core of most multi-player wargames.  A lot of those games try to pretend to be something else (ala Twilight Imperium)...but it all comes down to diplomacy.  Why bother with a game like TI3, just play Diplomacy instead...there's more action.  If I could get a group of 7 to play a 6-7 hour game of backstabbing and player elimination, this would be in my top 5.

RATING: 8
Awesome wooden blocks and a deluxe hard board map. The game follows the usual Diplomacy rules. A lot of discussions going on on who will support whom and who will be back stabbed. Decisions are written down for moving each army / navy block and revealed at the same time for all players involved. Some will be puzzled by the results others surprised and a few betrayed. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I love this game.  The designer said he took elements of two of my other "10" games: Chess and Poker.  It has the strategy of Chess and the player interaction of Poker.

This is the game that led me into Eurogames and Ameritrash.  It has elements of both (balanced game play, no luck, wooden components (my first version) AND player elimination, war and conquest).

The best game invented in the 20th century.  It has been around long enough and still has a big following to show it isn't a fad.

The design is genius.  They key to remember in this game; it is just a game.  You can't take it too seriously if someone backstabs you; just think of it like a bad roll of the dice, and move on.

It is best played with seven, so it has a unique status as a "Strategy" game with me.  It is my favorite specialized strategy game in that you need the right crowd, the right time, and the right amount.

It is an excellent game to play on-line, as it was often played as a mail game long before the internet came around.

RATING: 6
Simple but deep game of alliances and negotiation; I admire the mechanisms of the game.  But it is too dependent on backstabbing; the game’s free-form alliances seem somewhat broken.  If a strong alliance sticks together for most of the game, they are not very beatable, and there seems little incentive to backstab due to overlong game length, since games almost always end before two players are left. The game also takes too long to negotiate with everyone, and the “bouncing” mechanic can get frustrating and lead to many stalemates.  Not a very playable game in face-to-face gaming due to game length and player elimination (it is far better via email, but very intense).

RATING: 2.5
Eerily, (and I can't really explain how this came about) when push came to shove, and my friends and I pulled out the knives, we were all either emotionally bothered by the impact it had on us, or on other players at the table. Something happened. Despite the fact that we knew going in NOT to take anything personal, it stopped feeling like a game, and started feeling like what would happen if people were really acting this way.

We didn't like causing each other to feel that way seriously, even though it was just because of things going on in the game. I've realized something about games from playing Diplomacy, that "it's just a game" only carries so far. Even though I really, really appreciate the uniqueness and structure of this game, I'll be surprised if I can really bring myself to play it again. I used to be the person who said "If your friendship takes a hit over a game, what kind of friendship was it really?" It's just, in a way, it stopped feeling like a game. And it just wasn't the kind of game I ever wanted to play again.

RATING: 4
Fair. Unfortunately it is not consistently interesting across the length of the game.  It is best at the beginning, when the powers are more-or-less equal and the negotiation is sharp.  After a few powers have been eliminated, the opportunities for negotiation are cut at least in half and often more: usually the lines are drawn and obvious, but you still have to play it out or accept a draw.  The game system itself is perfectly-suited for the beginning of the game, where it should be simple so as not to overcomplicate the diplomatic aspects, but towards the end of the game it is too simple to hold up on its own.

RATING: 7
Diplomacy is a very good game of negotiation.

In order to win you need the other players' support, so you'll spend your time trying to draw plans and make deals with them, while keeping an eye on who they talk to and what they seem to be planning. Will you really be able to trust them or will you end up with a knife on your back? Your negotiation skills will decide it.

The downsides? It plays best only with exactly 7 players and it eats too much time, 4+ hours. But if both conditions are fulfilled, this is a game that really shines.

RATING: 6
Can be amazing, but any game will lead to at least some of the players spending the best part of the game doing very little, and it can drag on, so should always be played with a timer for the negotiation phase (10 minutes is about right). =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love and hate this game in equal measure. I find playing it a real rollercoaster and no other game has pi**ed me off as much.

But then I keep coming back for more. It's the way the game draws you in. There is an emotional involvement because of the very direct player interaction and their inter-reliability. You can't win without the help of others but these others are also trying to win for themselves.

It's a simple area control 'war game'. But the real meat of the game is in the diplomatic negotiations and the eventual lying and back stabbing that follows. There are not many tenser moments in gaming than when the orders are revealed.

I'm learning to be a better loser.

RATING: 6
As a game, Diplomacy could rate a 9 in my book- simply because it is a "pure game", in that it relies completely on the players, while the only luck that exists comes from the fact that your fate rests in the balance of the other players' intentions. Not many games live up to their title as well as this classic. 

Unfortunately, because of the negative side effects regarding diplomatic engagement,  necessary backstabbing, and very long playtime (6+ hours), some are put off by this experience. Many friends are lost over this game, which is also why I bring my rating down. The game can be a fun or miserable experience, depending on the nature of the players and what happens to them. But a game like this deserves at least one try by everyone!

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b][i]Diplomacy[/i][/b] is a no-luck war game that requires strategy, negotiation, and (often) backstabbing to win. It's a long game (5+ hours), and it basically requires 7 people to play. Consequently, games are rare and provide experiences to be treasured. Because of this, Diplomacy is not a game so much as it is an event. The hours of play leading up to a moment of strategic brillance and, perhaps, a betrayal make every game memorable. Diplomacy deserves a higher rank, but it sits here because of the difficulty of getting it played.

Many have maligned Diplomacy because of the back-stabbing element. The synopsis of this is that the game allows players to make non-binding agreements and then people sometimes get mad when other players do not follow through on the agreements. If people cannot handle this in a light-hearted way, then they shouldn't play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The classic negotiation game - players are the heads of European nations around the time of WWI - each player secretly make deals with any and all other players to oppose, support, and back stab and all is then revealed in your written orders when your real intentioms become clear.

The game requires a dedicated goup and a long time to play. It can suffer from the usual Risk game problem of king making although turtling is less of an issue.

If you have the time and the group - excellent.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I think this game has been eclipsed by others that use its diplomacy, non-dice rolling mechanic.  Even though I like the principles of the game in general you need too many players to play it and the game is so darned stressful that I don't find myself wanting to play often.

I find that the game plays much better by e-mail and actually plays the best online with people you don't know--having plenty of time for negotiations (days) means that calculations are less chaotic and stabs are better planned and less emotional--the game takes on a little bit more logic.

Something like Game of Thrones completely fires Diplomacy for me.

Finally, I don't know what else to say other than that there are some poor design problems like the common stalemate positions in the game.  I suppose you could argue that all the players usually know about them so it's not a problem but I still think it is a problem.  Add in the unbalanced starts which I am normally fully in support of.  Some players in a vastly better position than others and game design has to come into question.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I possess two version : one used copy with the plastic ancre and star pieces and the latest Hasbro/Avalon Hill/Wizards of the Coast edition.

This a game that needs two things in order to succeed : dedication and a tough skin. Dedication because learning it takes a long time, the games are long and you have to be an active participant throughout.

A tough skin because it's very easy to feel betrayed, frustrated and generally being picked on by the other. Keeping it cool, detached, unemotionnal is a requirement if you want to be able to enjoy a game.

Not everybody can play this.

RATING: 6
We played this a lot back in high school, but we were never able to finish an entire game due to curfew. Years before, a family friend gave me his copy of Diplomacy. This was the Games Research, Inc., edition from 1971. However, the copyright was 1959. Plenty of good memories, although the games were incomplete. We even ventured out to creating our own variants, such as "Fantasy Diplomacy", which was scribbled out with permanent markers on giant butcher paper. Nostalgia rating: "8".

It's just too hard to get seven players together these days, who are willing to spend 6+ hours back stabbing each other, just to get a stalemate?

It is a landmark design: no luck; winning is based on pure skill based on negotiating, manipulating, and collaborating. Do I want to spend 6+ hours scheming and clawing my way to victory? I'm really not that good of a liar, or a negotiator, and I don't like to piss people off, so this doesn't work for me. Current rating: "6".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Unquestionably a classic piece of game design, this often works better in theory than it does in practice. Ostensibly this is a negotiation game, the problem is, there is little that can be usefully negotiated with a liar. This means that rather than being a game, Diplomacy is really a metagame that essentially consists of the players collectively deciding who, at the table, will be the next player to be picked on and eliminated. When this happens, there's little that you can do, no amount of argument or skillful tactical play will save you and unless you survive the first round of eliminations then Diplomacy is no game at all. Small wonder that some resort to cheating, either approaching a game with a pre-arranged alliance with a trusted friend or playing more than one nation in a pbem game under different aliases.
As you can probably tell by now, I'm sharply conflicted as to the virtues of the standard game. I've had some of my finest gaming moments at Diplomacy, in amongst hours of boredom and misery. I'm no fan of the standard map as it tends too easily to stalemate, some of the small map variants (see 'Hundred Variant' for a good example) eradicate the above issues and so represent a huge improvement, as well as removing the obvious practical problems (the need for 7 players and a playing time so insane that the vast majority of games end as some form of draw). At this stage of my gaming life I'm pretty much done with Dip, but new players should try it, a few times at least.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Some of my most fond gaming memories are based around Diplomacy. I can remember playing games of it at college, where we would all submit turns twice a day and discuss moves during classes. I can remember multiple other games where we would show up at someone's house and play until the wee hours of the morning, getting angry at each other for betrayals and then immediately offering to ally again as soon as the moment was right.

I can also remember that I've literally never finished a game. I've also had people who got mad enough to not talk to me for days afterwards, and while I'm sure the majority of that blame is on me, I can't help but feel that the game brings it out of people as well.

It's an experience that I think everyone should have at least once, but I honestly don't think it's a very good game. Mechanically it's nothing incredible, and other games can fill a similar niche in far less time.

In short, I look back on Diplomacy fondly, but I would not recommend it as a good game, and would also be unlikely to play it again.

RATING: 10
Wow!

I never thought it would happen, but I've found a second 10 to go along with the one I gave to Settlers.

I can't say enough about this game.  The level of player interaction is off the charts.  The game isn't played on the board it is played when you play with the minds of the other players.  The tension right before orders are revealed and the sometimes jaw-dropping moments are truly priceless gaming moments.  

Just a note about the playtime and the bitter feelings which are the two most common complaints.  This game is in its element in play by email where it truly shines.  This perfectly suits people like myself who have a family and can't easily get people together for one hour of gaming let alone 8-10 hours.  Secondly, please play this game with the right people.  Don't play with your spouse.  Don't play with anyone who is prone to not being able to separate the game from reality (they will see your in-game stabs as an indictment on your real-life character).  Playing online tends to solve this problem as well.

Do yourself a favor and go play Diplomacy online - NOW!

RATING: 10
A masterpiece and perhaps my favorite game of all time.  The game is about human dynamics and human nature - pure strategy and negotiation with no luck (other than perhaps choosing your starting power).  The rules are remarkably simple and logical, and the simultaneous movement mechanic not only offers exciting gameplay, but also much more realistic than turn-based war or civilization games.

The game is at its best and most delicious in the first several turns - as everyone is still in the game and still has dreams of world domination.  The greatest thing about the game is that even as a small power with just 1-2 supply centers left, if you negotiate wisely and shrewdly, you can still maneuver in the game to regain influence and power, and perhaps a chance to win the game.

While postal / mail games are popular, I greatly prefer face to face games since the time commitment is actually more manageable - face to face games only usually take a day or a weekend, while online games that span months end up feeling like a second job with offers sometimes written like contract legalese.

Now, I understand the game has a reputation, but I believe they are unwarranted and comes from playing the game incorrectly.  I've hosted and GM'ed countless all-day face to face games, and we've  never had anyone leave the house with any fewer friendships than they arrived with.  As I tell all players at the beginning - Diplomacy is a game - what happens in the game stays in the game; nothing is personal.

Moreover, I do believe you can win this game without backstabbing.  The fact is, I have done so myself.  You need to work with other people in order to win.  And you don't have to make any promises that you can't keep.  The best players know that the meta-game is just as important (or more) as game strategy, tactical play, and transactional stabs.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
**NOSTALGIA RATING** 

This is a fantastic, lovely game. It enthralled me for years as a kid. Loved the interplay, the intrigue, the betrayals, the strategy, the cunning, the screw ups, the blown orders, and my first run at a programmed command structure in a board game. 

Very long with a full boat, wonderful for play by mail games, will consume you if you play it well. 

Direct confrontation and treachery is required to win...no ifs, ands, or buts about it. You can not win without turning on someone at some point since every country has multiple fronts to defend quickly...aside from the Witches that is.

I've always called Briton and Turkey the Witches for some reason (it had something to do with the corners...). Boil and Toil...You have 3 dark prophecies coming Hamlet. 

**THIS GAME IS NOT FOR EVERYONE*** Be warned, but it was my only "10" at one time in my life. Simple rules, tons of straight strategy, wonderful double think and 'reading' of players, offers, and the straight board 'truth'.

RATING: 7
This is a long game but brilliant. There is no luck involved at all and once you learn how to submit your orders then the game blossoms. It becomes a game of trying to predict others movements... find a balance between advancing and holding a defensive position. There's nothing better than holding a defensive position and stopping some one cold in there tracks..... But nothing worse than holding when nobody tries to attack you and you could have advanced.
Add the this the ability to support other people's troups and have their armies support you, and you end up with fragile alliances that could fall at a moments notice. You need friends to grow but grow to big and your friends will turn against you. 
Remember this is just a game and it will be fun.... very cutthroat.

RATING: 8
Really want the old AH version.  Needing seven players can be a drag, and woe to the guy who gets Italy.  Best played over cheese, sausage, and beer.

Anyways, the goal of diplomacy is to be the first nation to control 18 supply centers.  To do this, you must, through sheer force of numbers (two pieces > one piece; three pieces > two).  And to do THIS you must negotiate, intimidate, threaten, backstab, double- and triple-cross your opponents.  No dice in this game.

RATING: 9
Diplomacy was the first game that I played that really sold me on board gaming.  Sure I had played quite a few board games previous to this, but they were mostly of the Sorry variety.  I had also played a significant amount of roleplaying games like board games, but I *knew* that they were meant to be played differently, I just hadn't figured out how yet.

But Diplomacy really opened up to me how a good board game could envigorate in me a great sense of excitement and provide a fun way for me to socialize with my friends.  While I understand that this game has its flaws overall, its heavy reliance on negotiation and trust really fits my mode of play, and is almost as-perfect as I want.

RATING: 7
Diplomacy, classic that it is, is also, in terms of fun or enjoyability, entirely dependent on the personalities of the people playing. A genial group might have a good time, others may not—

—and something that I have found crucial to realize about the game is that it's ability to create tension and stress is strongly correlated to the length of time allowed for negotiation. An online game with 24-hour adjudication periods is intensely stressful, whereas an in-person game with five minutes for negotiation can almost be comical.

Still, for all the cleverness of the map and mechanics, I prefer negotiation games with more compelling mechanics or deeper theme—and that is entirely a personal thing.

RATING: 10
A perfect 10. You don't need much to play.. the map, the pieces, and a lot of straight faces and the willingness to backstab your best friend, or your life partner, or even your own mother. This game is vicious and is mostly all about negotiation and backstabbing any allies you have before they backstab you. If you don't enjoy crushing an opponents best laid plans, or even watching it happen, this game isn't for you. But it's worth a try, just don't blame me if your gaming group breaks up afterwards... :D

For me, this game represents the pinnacle of psychological game design.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
At this point in my life, there are other games that I'd rather play but Diplomacy was once one of my very favorite games.  The simple movement and supply mechanics (no stacking, no recordkeeping, no factors) complement perfectly the real point of the game: free-wheeling negotiation and scheming.  Some people are put off by the malevolent duplicity necessary to play this game.  Personally, I love the tingling of anticipation and fear as "stabs" are being plotted.  Not all of the countries are equally likely to win, but they all have their own personality and can be fun to play.  The game can last a LONG time and sometimes bogs down or becomes stalemated with sustained best play.  In face-to-face play, the winner is often the player who can establish an early lead and then simply outlast the stamina of the others.  For these reasons, the game is better suited to PbEM in many ways.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
This classic game of pure negotiation. Players represent one of the seven "Great Powers of Europe" (Great Britain, France, Austria, Germany, Italy, Russia or Turkey) in the years prior to World War I. Players instruct each of their units by writing a set of "orders." The outcome of each turn is determined by the rules of the game. There are no dice rolls or other elements of chance. With its incredibly simplistic movement mechanics fused to a significant negotiation element, this system is highly respected by many a gamer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.8
One of the classic games of all time:  easy to learn, easy to play, hard to master, and impossible to win.  Yes, all the rumors are true:  this game WILL descend, and rapidly, into backstabbing, rancor, ill will, and even destroyed friendships.  It's also as fun as hell and something I wish I could play more often (a problem, insofar as you generally need seven players).

The design is pretty timeless; it's not that much to look at, unless you have some fancy edition, but it's not the looks that makes Diplomacy appealing; it's the winner-take-all fiendishness of the play.  It will bum you out if you let it, but if you're in the mood for some pure cutthroat winner-take-all strategy, there's nothing better.

RATING: 7
A very nice game with a brilliantly simple design. It's very straightforward: you can move, support, build or convoy or hold. All moves are done simultaneously. That's it and that is the beauty of the game. Diplomacy is definitely key in the game. The only problem is the length of the game; we could've fitted in many other games which are equally enjoyable. Second is the tension level is so high, that you either love it or hate it. I was engrossed in the competitiveness of the game but my partner wasn't used to such high level tension for such a long period of time. This game practically screams at you to backstab someone, and it is something that is bound to happen sooner or later, especially when the timing is just perfect. Regardless, it's a game I will never suggest due to it's length, but will always be happy to play again.

RATING: 10
I consider Diplomacy to be the purest, most elegant, and most ruthlessly efficient - or is that efficiently ruthless? - board game of all time. If Texas Hold'em is the Cadillac of card games, then Diplomacy is the Bentley of board games. Your success is solely in your hands, without the worry of getting "screwed by the dice" or otherwise crushed by chance.

If you are willing to betray your closest friend or significant other, if you are able to lie with a smile on your face and then sleep well that night, then Diplomacy is the game for you.

RATING: 7
Among the greatest games ever produced.  This is not strictly a boardgame, it is more a game of competitive personal interaction.  It requires the abilities to nurture trust and cooperation, to deceive, lie, beg, cajole, to create and decipher intrigues, and to weigh risk and capitalize on opportunities when they appear, often by a vicious stab of your erstwhile ally.  (This is not for everyone!)  And, despite the relatively simple game mechanics, it demands a great deal of tactical prowess to succeed.

For a few short years, I practically lived and breathed Diplomacy, especially playing postally.  It's been 15 years or so since I've played in a face-to-face game and I'll almost certainly never do so again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Best with experienced players.  One of those games simple to learn yet not so easy to play to victory all the time.  You need good people skills for this game – must be able to convince others to do you a favor as ally or bidding as you duped minion.  I won Best Germany in one convention that France allied with me.  I lost quickly as Italy in game I thought I could trust Austria in.  Life goes on.

I have played in tournaments at gaming convention – it has the advantage of no consequences for backstabbing later on (complete stranger easier to deceive then guy who drove you to convention and who just might leave with out you).  PBEM is okay but it tends to have high dropout rate.  Best when turns are a few days apart (if not weekly) so the hashing of deals and conspiracies go the full course.

If you play with newbies try to help them out.  Don’t just dogpile on them with your buddies and turn them off to a good game.  Good Dip players take time to develop – need to build up the calluses on the old ego.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This cross between a wargame and a negotiation game is the best in its genre, by far.

In order to win this game, you have to do well  in both war games and negotiation games. You need to be clever in manouvering the few pieces you own in order to make even a small gain. But, far more important, you need get other players to move THEIR pieces so that they help YOU to win the game!

The game can be played by mail, but is best played face-to-face. This way, a game can easily take eight hours and still feel like a fast-paced game, without a single minute of 'down-time'! It is a turmoil of little chats, strategic discussions, and tactical plotting. You have to try to influence the other players in doing the right things, while constantly be aware that players may not tell the truth and are scheming to backstab you. And finally, you have to complete your complex orders under the pressure of the clock.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I really appreciated this game when I was younger, but I don't ever see myself playing it again.

Firstly, playing less than the full 7 players is so much less satisfying, and finding 7 people happy to spend an entire day on a game can be extremely difficult.

Secondly, not only does it take over six hours, but the game will usually be effectively over for two or three of players within the first hour and a half.

Thirdly, it can take a long time before players no longer with a chance of winning are eliminated, and these players have usually lost a lot of interest, their actions affecting the outcome for other players but not themselves.

Also, there is significant mismatch between starting countries.  Players usually have preferences, but Austria, Germany and Italy have significantly lower win rates.

This is a great game, and the skill of how you interact with other players is brilliant, together with weighing up your strategic position.  I now enjoy playing games for more than just those reasons however, and prefer games where everyone can enjoy some time together.  People might be sick of hearing the words "victory point", but it does solve the problem of player elimination, and keeps games to a length where serious gamers, and not so serious gamers can enjoy it.

RATING: 4

Your game is over. If you want a modern version of this game with far more balance in regards of this player loss issue, then play Game of Thrones - it is obviously based on diplomacy but greatly modified to cover all the issues, amount of diplomacy itself slightly reduced (but still necessary) and game time itself shortened 1.5-2 times. This is a very old game and it probably was very good back in the days. Not recommended.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Sometimes I find the rating system on BGG a little wonky.  Giving Diplomacy a 2 seems harsh.  However, based on what a 2 in BGGs rating system means, it's the closest to how I feel about the game.

Diplomacy is a war/negotiation game set in the years leading up to WWI.  The 7 major European countries are represented on the board, which is in fact a map of Europe.  To win, a player has to control 18 specially marked provinces on the board.  Every country starts with three of these provinces, except Russia, which starts with four.  In order for anyone to do well at all they are going to have to form alliances with other players.  However, these alliances are fragile at best as players never know how much to trust each other, and are always looking for an opportunity to turn on their allies when it's most advantageous.

Diplomacy is an incredibly well thought out game.  All countries have more-or-less even strength to start, and are immediately threatened by at least two other neighbouring countries.  I really appreciate and applaud the game for its design.

I hate long games.  I just don't want to invest hours upon hours of time to one game.  Despite this I really wanted to try Diplomacy, which has a reputation of going long.  Now I've played it, and my desire to play has been quenched - forever.  This is why I rate Diplomacy a 2.  It's because a 2 indicates that I never want to play the game again.  For one thing, the game had gone seven hours before the first (and as it turned out only) player was eliminated.  A big reason for the length is that you can only go so far before getting bogged down in stand-offs with other countries'  military units.  These impasses are frequent, and slow progress down considerably.  Second, you have to lie to other players and stab them in the back.  This is known about the game, and my group even discussed it before we started.  We reminded ourselves that it's only a game, so don't get too upset when someone turns on you.  However, when I stabbed my friend in the back, who thought we had a rock-solid alliance, he was angry.  I didn't like making him feel that way.  I also didn't like betraying his trust in me, even though it was only within the game.  I've realized that if a game causes negative emotions I'm just not into it.  I sold my copy of Intrigue after one play for this same reason.

So, I doubt I'll ever play Diplomacy again.  For the reasons mentioned I can't recommend the game either.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This is hands down the best game I have ever played. Unfortunately, it comes with a huge caveat: you need to play with the right people. Playing with people who are oversensitive is simply a recipe for disaster, and you need to recognize this going in if the game is going to be any fun at all.

This isn't a game you can just take off the shelf and play at a game night - plan in advance, gather your six least sensitive friends, and spend the entire day. It will be worth it.

The rules are simple and the strategy deep, but it is the negotiation that truly makes this game shine.

RATING: 9
Absolutely love this game.  I can play it with friends, and with strangers, but usually not with a mix.  The chief reason for this being: I love the balance of this game.  The large push to escape the "random chance" element that is always so prevalent in board and card games.  Instead, there is a heavy reliance on your own social skills, strategic wisdom, and even your overall willpower.  However, when there is a combination of strangers and close friends in a single game, I feel like it unbalances the diplomatic element.  Some people know others well already, and so there are all ready unbalanced relations set up in advance.  But maybe that mirrors real-life diplomacy.  I'm probably just bitter from always being betrayed my closest friends in this game haha.  Definitely a favorite though.  It would be a 10, if it weren't for the replay value.  After 10 or so games with the same people, they start to replicate past games.  Needs a changing crowd.  But love it nonetheless.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
My only pure ten, and this game is so superb, "it goes up to eleven." If I have the number of people (and for me that even includes six or five with a variant board), I will always suggest it first, and I will always chose it over another game. Why? Of all the boardgames I've played, this is the one that had the most profound psychological impact on me while playing, the one that challenges me the most intellectually, the one I learn the most from playing - both about strategy, about other human beings, and about myself. And because it is the only game that I've ever played where the metagame is drawn so far into the forefront of the action, it actually feels paradoxically HONEST to play it (that is to say - since ALL games have this metagame layer - Diplomacy is the only one that acknowledges and incorporates this layer as honestly and directly into the play itself, so that to play it correctly is to play the metagame as much as the game. Most games require players to deny the metagame, but it's always there, just below the surface of the actions, affecting our choices and perceptions. At least in a game of Dip, I know players are going to try to manipulate me and each other openly. To me that honesty is a kind of relief.)

To be a master of games, a worthy opponent, an ubermensch of strategy, is to acknowledge there is no game free of metagame, and that ultimately all games involve negotiation, even if that negotiation is nonverbal/unspoken. Mastery of Diplomacy, of it's elements of deception, politicking, manipulation, and backstabbing, and even more importantly, mastering all of the above happening TO YOU, and still being able to walk away with a smile, realizing that is IS all just a game, are essential elements for mastery of all other games. Hell I'll go a step farther and say mastery of these things is necessary to live life happily and well. 

Or if you dislike my hyperbolic rhetoric, let me put it another way. To borrow from clearclaw, Diplomacy, for me, more so than any other game, seems to highlight "the intersection of the mechanically deterministic aspects of games with human ambiguity."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
BREAKDOWN OF SCORE:

Cleverness and game mechanics of the game: 9
Length of game, combined with the horrible sickness-to-stomach feeling that I feel throughout EVERY moment of EVERY time i've played this: 1

Average: 5

I'm just don't have the fortitude to endure this game, although the diceless and cooperative-while-simultaneously-backstabbing element of the game is very unique and clever.  Also another complaint:  the cooperative win condition of the game enabled me to win my first game (played during my junior year of high school, on the only day of classes i ever skipped) when I, playing as England, realized that between myself and Russia (whom I'd never interacted with or cooperated with for the whole game) could win the game simply by declaring an alliance, because between us we had enough regions to win.  A very lackluster finish.  (but of course, I couldn't avoid the opportunity to win it as soon as possible, given how sick I felt at backstabbing by best friend as Germany).

A final note: I've seen so many people get so upset at people lying to them, that the bad feeling has persisted beyond the game.  I know that lying/deception is a part of many games that I really enjoy (e.g. poker), but I guess the directness and the extent to which you must lie and betray an ally in this game for the ploy to be successful makes the consequences a little more severe.    This is the one game I think would be much better to play with strangers than with friends.  (although, on second thought, Poker too might often be better played with strangers)...
For this final issue, another point is deducted from that 5.

RATING: 3.5
I respect the elegance and purity of this game. However, its probably the most personality-sensitive game ever made so it can either go very well (rarely) or (commonly) can piss of friends and people you've only just met. And this is my biggest problem with Diplomacy - its more metagame than game. If you try to play with people who you don't know so well and they're all friends then you're just going to lose no matter what. Furthermore, it gets boring with repeated plays inside the same group. Finally, it goes on far too long, especially for those players who are eliminated early and have to play a filler while they wait for the main game to end (or deadlock). I definitely won't play this again unless by PBEM.

RATING: 5
The game works (there's nothing dysfunctional about it) although it was really designed for maximal player count: both the talking and the simultaneous resolution of 20+ orders make each turn last very, very long and player elimination must be taken in count so that players have a plan B once they're out of the game. It's just unlikely that I'll ever get 7 suitable players committing to the prospect of that game for 7-12 hours (possibly less for a few of them), relegating Diplomacy to the realm of play-by-mail computer games. I also emit some doubts concerning the balance of the map.

You might wonder what kind of prejudiced snob I am for talking about "suitable players". Well, let's just say that on top of the "must like negotiation/wargames" filter, there is this subset of people who have a hard  time separating the gaming space from the rest of their life and tend to take things quite personally. Diplomacy is longer than any film you've probably seen and is all player-driven narrative, no filler. You will grow to care about that nation you've been impersonating for the last 4 hours and there will be betrayal at crucial points. If you can't handle the thought of your best friend or your significant other casually blowing up what you've been building with care for god knows how long or have demonstrated anything close to being a sore loser, I would highly recommend you stay as far away from Diplomacy as you can.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

I had a series of games with a group of friends and we really got into it and nobody ended up hating anyone else. I think it's important that we kept up the disconnect between our real selves and our in-game roles; it was always "France, I want to talk to you", rather than "Dave, I want to talk to you". Given the amount of personal interaction involved, I could see how this could easily get messy if people have the wrong attitude to the game and take things personally.

Getting enough players to commit to the time required is a bit difficult, unfortunately, or I'd have played it more.

RATING: 7
An absolute classic game.  It does suffer from the problem that it takes forever to play; I haven't played this face-to-face in many years because of this.  Playing this over the internet or PBM has other advantages too, as it allows more time for diplomacy and hides who is conspiring with who, and you'll often need a lot of time to plan your moves to find the most reliable strategy.  The game does have weaknesses in that it is too easy to set up stalemate lines, and other well-known deficiencies in the map such as Venice and Trieste neighbouring each other.  I'm not sure if I'll play this game much in the future, but it will always be a favourite of mine.

RATING: 10
[size=9][b][c]OVERALL REVIEW:     :star::star::star::star::star:[/c][/b][/size]
[size=8]Quick and easy to teach. Good classroom game. The best diplomatic negotiation game ever. Every game play is different. Easily re-themed/expanded with custom components and maps.
[color=green]My favorite negotiation game.[/color][/size]

[size=9][c]RULES SIMPLICITY:   :star::star::star::star::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Simultaneous order resolution requires consulting the rules[/size]
[c]QUICK SETUP:        :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Minimal setup to determine initial territory ownership[/size]
[c]SHORT GAME LENGTH:  :nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Four hours or more, possibly days or weeks in length[/size]
[c]MAXIMUM STRATEGY:   :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]The enemy of your enemy is your friend[/size]
[c]MINIMUM LUCK:       :star::star::star::star::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]You are lucky if all of your moves actually succeed on a turn[/size]
[c]PLAYER INTERACTION: :star::star::star::star::star:[/c]
[size=8]Players can assist or compete with you in every move[/size]
[c]COMPONENT QUALITY:  :star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar:[/c]
[size=8]Depends on version. Favorites 1961-76 wooden or 1999 Hasbro metal pieces[/size]

[size=8]You really do feel like you are negotiating peace and conflict for your country[/size]
