[size=8]5 or more players[/size]

[b][c]MY PLAYS:[/c][/b]
[size=8]online by email
in-person with 5 players single session
in-person with 3,4,5,6,7 players, one turn per day/week
in-person as judge for 7,9,12 players, one turn per week[/size][/size]

1961-76 wooden edition:

[ImageID=661788 small]

1999 Hasbro metal edition:

[ImageID=1941609 small]

[size=10]Diplomacy was created in 1954 and is arguably the archetype for all negotiation games ever since. There are no dice, and there is only one player unit per occupied territory. At the beginning of a turn, a non-binding negotiation phase allows players to discuss possible movements and support among one another, publicly and/or privately. At the conclusion of the negotiation phase, each player secretly submits their final binding movements via written orders. All player movements are revealed simultaneously and resolved with a simple set of rules for the resulting chain reactions.

[color=#3388cc][b]One cannot be told what it is like to play Diplomacy. You must play it to understand.[/b][/color]

[size=10]Anyone [i]can[/i] play Diplomacy, but not everyone [i]should[/i]...[/size]

[inlineimg]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/inlineimg] You cannot be a sore loser.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] You are likely to lose, and possibly very quickly.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] You might lie to other players.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] Other players might lie to you.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] You might backstab your friends.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] Your friends might backstab you.
[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] You will have a constant level of paranoia.

[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/delete.png[/INLINEIMG] If you [i]do not[/i] find any of the conditions above to be enjoyable in a game, then you will find Diplomacy to be the worst game you have ever encountered. Worse than Monopoly, Dodge Ball, or Slug Bug Yellow. If you have only heard about Diplomacy, you will give it a BGG rating of 2 to 8 based on your perceptions. If you accidentally play Diplomacy, you will give it a BGG rating of 1 and tell everyone that the game is horrible.

[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/accept.png[/INLINEIMG] If you [i]are okay[/i] with the conditions mentioned above, then you are likely to enjoy the game, and will probably give it a BGG rating of 9 or 10.

It should be emphasized that the negotiation phase is non-binding. Any player may propose any movement or support, and share any information about any other player's proposals. A player's actual orders for the current turn are not final until they have been written and submitted via written orders. Any unit in the game that does not have an order is considered to be "holding" its ground in defense. Invalid orders, which happen more frequently than expected, are discarded. 

There is a constant buildup of suspense throughout a turn, until all of the orders have been submitted and are revealed. When playing in-person, the level of negotiation and paranoia increases dramatically and players retain a great nostalgia for epic plays, years after the fact.

[INLINEIMG]https://cf.geekdo-static.com/images/icons/silkicons/information.png[/INLINEIMG] [color=#3388cc][b]Hardcore/Professional Play:[/b][/color]

With the proper guidance, and even re-themed, I have read of many successful classroom diplomacy games as demonstrations of negotiation, support and concession. We have found that the game works best when players can commit to a slow game with one turn per day or week. A game usually lasts ten to sixteen turns, which can be two weeks or several months, depending on turn frequency. 

There can be, but not required, one neutral participant that acts as Game Master/Judge and accepts all player turn orders via email or by hand. 

All players should have access to an online or printed photo or drawing of the map that shows the current status of owned/occupied territories and all units in play. Players can negotiate any time and any place they desire, as long as they submit their final orders prior to the turn deadline. 

At the appointed time, all players should gather in person or video conference, around the official map board and playing pieces. When the final orders are revealed and resolved, each player can experience the jubilation, dispair, triumph and defeat, and see the emotions on each other's faces as alliances are formed or betrayed.

[color=#3388cc][b]Variations?[/b][/color]

There are multiple map variations for different numbers of players. The Diplomatic Pouch and similar sites have many maps to suit your tastes.

[color=#3388cc][b]Pimp it?[/b][/color]

When playing with more than seven players, you have to create additional playing pieces. I have used FIMO clay to create ships and infantry units. There are  many colors and you can cut simple meeple shapes with an xacto knife and bake the clay to harden it. The clay does not shrink and will maintain its bright colors, unless you overcook them. Or you could acquire multiple sets of Diplomacy and paint extra pieces to meet your color needs.
[/size]

RATING: 10
[2010/01/01] +4 plays before recording


disclaimer: i don't actually "own" this game, but i have a great web server i play it on...
-
in fact i could not imagine playing the board game version again (i played the board game version once, and i like the web version FAR better)
Why?
Diplomacy is a mammoth of a game... the board game version would require too much table space for too long, and I dont think you could ever schedule 7 people not in college/retired to spend all day playing 1 game

In addition the web server adjuncates the moves atomatically; it provides the perfect interface for truly secret diplomatic messages; it allows you to view that games movement history; and it lets you view the history of all the games you've played.

To top all of that off, the web game is far superior for all the players that lose the game early on... (they aren't just stuck there as a non-participant, but they can view all of the remaining moves at thier leisure)
______________

So now that you know why the web version is better than the boardgame version... here is why Diplomacy is the greatest game of all time:

First lets do the book vs movie comparison/analogy... A book typically takes longer than a movie thus allowing you greater time to learn the characters, the plot, and the intricate details. A movie can be good for what it is, but I MUCH more enjoy a good book than a good movie.

Diplomacy is the "book" of boardgames... you will not finish diplomacy in one sitting... every year is it's own chapter, and the feeling of the game can dramatically change at any time

like chess, this game requires prediction of your opponents moves... but unlike chess you start with 6 opponents; you all move at the same time; and you can influce your opponents moves.

The real magic of diplomacy is the unique skill set that you use to win... With chess: its all strategy... with catan and other eurogames: it's a mix of tactics, strategy, and sometimes random-chance... Diplomacy involves NO random chance and it only requires BASIC strategy and tactics to play, but they are both subserviant to the skill of diplomacy... and if you aren't certain what that means, let me describe it as the ability to influence others into a desired course of action. Nothing is sacred, and and any course of action is acceptible: You may lie, mislead, ignore, doublecross, join, backstab, plead, threaten, support, guide, accuse, plagerize, help, cower, or abstain.

In the truist sense you are playing each of your opponents... they are the game, and any information you can gain from them is valuable. the map simply serves as a way to measure your diplomatic success. (but if you are a strategist or a tactitian, it will help to complement and support your diplomatic skills).

The first game I played to completion took a total of 2-3 months (10-60 mins every other day on average). I played against a great gaming friend from home (he is one of the top diplomacy players on the web, with 10 or so active games at a time.) I did not let him know that he was playing against me, and it was such an incredible experience to play against such an talented opponent.

I highly recomend this game to anyone who meets this criteria:
*THICK SKINED (your best friend could crush you)
*RESPONSIBLE (you will need submit orders on a regular basis)
*GAMER (you should have a love for board games, and have a decent level of strategical and tactical ability)
*DEDICATED/RESPECTFUL (this is a game for 7 people... I have seen people last with "1 unit" up until the bitter end... even if you are losing, you need to respect your fellow gamers, and play your peices untill the game is over or untill you have no peices left)

if you are lacking in these areas, this game is not for you, if you meet this criteria, then i hope you can research/learn/and play this game...


I WANT to play this again, but i would prefer to play it with people i know over the web interface... I'm trying to influce people to play now

It's player elimination combined with an extremely long play time. There is no better way to spoil a good evening with friends. Or, even worse - plays where a player is only almost eliminated, but still forced to watch the game without having much to contribute. That is not only insuling to friends and/or guests to someone's house, it is also the opposite of what a board game should do.

There are other, much better games with similar elements, like Junta (because it offers different ways of escaping from a dreadful position, while it's also very humrous and has more exciting moments), Game of Thrones (because it's more thamtic, more random and has limited play time), or simply Risk (because it's short with missions).

RATING: 9
A classic that relies on the duplicitous nature of us all. No luck, pure negotiation and clear written instructions. No matter how casually a game begins within a couple of turns everyone will be sharpening their knives.

Personally I play this game in as relaxed an atmosphere as I can muster and luckily those I've played it with have been very reasonable and rational players.

Whilst I am aware that the game has very exact rules with regards unclear orders or ambiguous situations I would hate to play this with anyone who stuck rigidly to these. As far as I am concerned if a ghist was clear enough and others agree then that is good enough for me and my group.

RATING: 6
UPDATE: Traded at "Alphaspel" in Sweden. I really would like to play this one more time at least. But I see it hard find 7 people (optimum sweet spot apparently) who are prepered to play this Marathon game for 7 hours in a row.

***

I never thought that I would get my copy of Diplomacy to the table. But I did - at least once I did.

After 3 hours "Germany" flipped and start yelling to us all just before he left the table for a poker game with some others playing at the same place. Of course, we had a great time and the game had been really tense and interesting up to this point. We were five remaining players that continued the pursiut of dominating Europe. But you know what, in 4 more hours nothing more exiting really happend. We tried to attack and make pacts, but it became a stale game with rather fixed borders. We ended the game after over 7 hours, that was before any official winning conditions was reached. We just choosed the leading player as the winner.

It started really fine and it provided a nice psychological drama, but in the end it just gave me headace. I think Imperial and Struggle of Empires have just as strong meta-game as Diplomacy, but they are more distinctive and have more paths and options.

In some aspects Diplomacy felt like a light game. But the the part of resolving combat/bouncing were rather complex at times.

RATING: 10
If you haven't played Diplomacy don't dare to call yourself a gamer! The game teaches you a lot about how people think and how the world works. This is the sum-of-all-games (IMHO).

Suggestions:

1) Only play with 7 players.
2) One game in an evening does not makes sense, thus: Play in a one-year-per-week concept, for instance at your work place.  See the social structures evolve. Backstabbing colleagues. Eavesdropping. It's all in the game! (One of the few times that the blurb on the box actually does not promise too much).
3) To maintain the administration and (essential strict) deadlines for handing in moves, use an on-line arbitrator robot ("judge").
4) Don't need to actually BUY the game. Just print a map and the rules. So, best game ever AND free. What more does a person want?

RATING: 8
THE classic strategy game.  Easy rules.  Play 3-4 players to learn. 7 is very tense from the start.  There are no dice or cards for luck -- everything is negotiations with the other players.  To do well you'll need to make alliances.  To win, you'll need to backstab your allies.  Can jeopardize friendships if taken too personally and not all personalities should play this game.  Excellent played possibly in somewhat abbreviated fashion as a classroom exercise grades 9-12 for world history and geo-politics understanding.

RATING: 4
Tons of interaction between players, however I feel like there is too much time spent doing nothing in this game. Negotiation and backstabbing rule the day, but they can take a LONG time to get through.  Even after orders are in, you need a flowchart to determine what goes where.  Play A Game of Thrones if you're looking for a game that just kept most of the good parts of Diplomacy.

RATING: 6
Enjoyed it years ago, I rather like diplomacy because of the negotiation involved. It is usually best to keep you treaties but I've been one to fake some people out at times think I at odds with an ally.

The primary problems are the number of players needed and the elimination factor along with the game length. Feel that later games such as Struggle of Empires, Pax Britainna cover the multi-player aspect better.

Also my rating reflects that I like the early and mid-game which I would rate a 9. However, I feel the game deteriorates as it goes along and I find the end game to be distinctly disappointing so I would rate that a 3. - Hence my 6 rating. 

The game would have been better to play a set or variable number of years, or to the first person to a say 10 or 12 centers - a good amount but not half the board.

RATING: 10
Here's the thing - this is not a game for everyone. But in the same way that Go and Chess have a simplicity and elegance, Diplomacy is along the same lines. The simplicity of mechanics and the need to convince others to go along with your plan make this a perfect game for me. But being a perfect game doesn't mean I want to play it exclusively. In fact, I haven't played this game in years. The only randomness in this game exists within the minds of the players. It can be brutal, when backstabbing or being backstabbed, and that's really the only downside. It can sting when you trust someone and it all falls apart. I am told that some friendships have been damaged by this game. Playing online with strangers offers a clean slate every time.

RATING: 8
By far the best concept for a game I have ever experienced.
With the right group of people this transcends boardgaming entirely and becomes a dark and twisted form of LARPing.

The elephant in the room is that in execution, Diplomacy takes FOREVER to play through.  I'm talking 6+ hours if you do it without breaks.  Potentially a week if you want to eat and sleep.  And you absolutely must have a full player count.

My recommendation is to set up a Diplomacy day weeks ahead of time, with lunch and dinner pre-set for delivery, and allow no one to leave.
Will it be a boardgame day or the setup for a murder mystery?  Why can't it be both?  I mean, by the end you'll likely want to murder your friends anyway...

RATING: 8
Keep your allies close, but your enemies closer.

I love this game. Everything about it is exactly up my alley. It is Risk, but instead of playing the game, you are "playing" the other players. It is all about having a silver-tongue and poisoning the minds of those else.

It unfortunately, takes forever to play (devote 4-7 hours depending on number of players, and even then, more if you have those who really want to win). You know how Mario Kart for the Nintendo64 destroys friendships?

This is the boardgame equivalent. Only those that can beckon the sociopaths and cold-blooded intentions inside of themselves will win this game.

RATING: 6
On the surface, this game is brilliant. It is the ultimate in zero-luck games. It's just you, a map of Europe in 1900, the opening position, and your opponents. After that's it's survival by your wits alone. Unfortunately, as this simulates the diplomatic efforts and command decisions that took place leading up to and including World War I, with no luck mechanic to blame when things go wrong, it gets pretty tense around the game table as nation after nation gets stabbed in the back or ganged up on. Not for the casual gamer or the faint of heart.  Beware this game's reputation: Where friendships go to die.

RATING: 4
It emulates World War I in a luckless game of simultaneous movement through written (secret) orders to armies and fleets. Has good strategic depth, but victory cannot be achieved without skills on diplomacy and deceit. The game also needs seven players to work well and, therefore, its sessions can take a lot of time. A classic that will appeal only to gamers that can handle well the mandatory "backstab-to-win", player elimination and, above all else, the exhaustive amount of time that it takes to be played.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Epic game of negotiation, deal-making and conflict. Players represent one of the seven Great Powers in the buildup to WWI. Each turn army and fleet orders are documented and revealed simultaneously to hold or move troops, allowing players to move their armies, take control of hotly contested supply centers (which expand armies), and displace opponents. Troop "support" from neighboring regions (provided by oneself or an ally) is key to winning conflicted regions. Support chain management and displacement of support are critical, but it is the timing of partnerships, when to ally, when to reneg on a deal, and the understanding and anticipation of other players' moves and motives that are key to winning
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Also, it is a frustrating game to play with people who have studied the stalemate positions on the board. I would much rather play it with people who haven't reverse-engineered the whole game. To keep the game fresh for me, I have specifically avoided such study.

RATING: 1.7
I probably couldn't count the number of miserable times I have had with this Diplomacy. The game itself has virtually no mechanics, so gameplay relies entirely on user interaction - before every turn players discuss with each other their plans for expansion and work deals out based on that. The way we played, everyone met each other one to one in private and exchanged their "strategies." What this boils down to is making empty promises, getting frustrated that other people break theirs, and way more time wasting than should be ever reasonable for a board game. Even worse, I have found that as time goes on people take the game more and more seriously and pretty much everyone gets angry and bitter towards one another. Not at all my idea of a fun board game night.

RATING: 6
Difficult game to rate because I recognize the brilliance of it. The rules are incredibly simple, but the gameplay allows a deep and unique experience. 

I actually look for interactive and cutthroat games, but I just feel the human element in this one is more than I care for. So much of the game is in the negotiation part of the game. It is intensely social in that way. Reading emotions and making deals is what sets it apart from many other board games. While I may enjoy that once in a while, it's not the self-contained logic puzzle with clearly defined player actions that I normally look for. It brings in the messy world of human relations and all of their emotional reactions.

In the end, it's a game that I will come back to on the right occasion with the right group of people, but not one I want to pull out every week.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A classic from my youth. No game does a better job of leveraging the power of persuasion and maximizing the impact of betrayal. Hence its perfectly fitting name. I played many games in high school and college; I liked playing Turkey most of all.

In high school we played a format where one person was the moderator, and the game was played out on photocopies of the map with the unit placements drawn on them. A turn lasted a few days, which gave you time to do your wheeling and dealing without other players' knowledge. Then you submitted your orders to the moderator, who resolved the turn and handed out the updated maps. A game would take a couple of months to play; this was a really fun format.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The first thing to remember is that Diplomacy is inherently unbalanced. Russia starts with 4 units. England has more fleets, but less armies. Austria, Germany and Italy have to contend 4 or 5 direct opponents compared to Turkey's 2. The second thing is that this game will take at least 6 hours, and that's with a moderator to keep time and hurry players along. There needs to be a place with multiple rooms so that people can deliberate their warplans without being overheard. The third thing is that this is not a wargame. There are four basic orders (each with their own nuances), but most of this game stems from having the better alliance and then possibly breaking it. This is the ultimate game to make friends fume and cry, and while that shouldn't put you off this game, it's definitely good to go in knowing that it's just a game! The game plays out in simultaneous turns with orders written down on a piece of paper and resolved once everyone has put in their orders or the time is up. This makes for exciting and tense play and most of the time, there isn't too much downtime as players will always be talking with each other or watching the outcome of everyone's orders. This is a very exciting must-play game for the right people if you can find seven mature players, a moderator (ideally), 6-8 hours of free time and a place with plenty of places to talk. Otherwise, it can be difficult to organise.

RATING: 9
This is my all-time favorite game. I would play it forever and always if I could. The complete lack of any random element makes this one of the purest strategy games I've played. The diplomatic/coercion element makes every game startlingly different.

There are really only two downsides to this game:

1. You must have EXACTLY seven people to play. Any fewer and the game gets ridiculously imbalanced. Any more and people have to be on teams. This CAN work, but nothing is worse than in-fighting on a team ruining your game.

2. While I am a big fan of asymmetrical gameplay, I feel that the starting positions for some countries are significantly stronger than others (*cough* France *cough*). This makes it hard to bring new people into the game, as they lack the acumen to turn that frown upside-down.

However, barring these two relatively minor complaints, this is my favorite game of always.

RATING: 6
A strange game indeed. I have it, read it, didn't get it, repacked it, forgot about it.
Seriously: I never fully understood the rules on breaking support etc. Too difficult to understand. And how about all that writing down orders... I don't really take to games that need a pen and paper. 
The negotiation and leaving dice behind is okay. But someone should invent an easier way of giving orders without need of a pen and paper. Furthermore I need rule clarification, major clarification (I can prove this game is to hard to interpret since even PBEM clubs need a referee to interpret what actually happens on the board.

Edit 6/2/08. Since yesterday we started a PBEM game complete with Gamemaster. That means that in all fairness to the game I need to adjust my rating since a 3 says one will never play the game again. Final decision may depend on the playability of the e-mail variant and I suppose factors as length etc.

29/02/2008 Ok, so now that I played PBEM Diplomacy, what is my verdict¦ I didn’t like it, and here is why. When I was still on the prowl for girls back in the day, I would write a lot of sms and e-mails to them with all sorts of garbage and indecent proposals of sorts. What I hated then is what I hate now: the waiting... Waiting for a reply when negotiating or waiting for a confirmation that someone (a temporarily ally for example) received your set of proposed orders, or not. Waiting for that ally to respond, hoping it's not a negative response since you only have an hour left for the deadline¦ All of this and more made me feel like I did when I was chasing after women back then: I loved the game: but hated the waiting. Like someone in our gaming group said: send mail F5 F5 F5 F5 F5, then phew¦ I think it says it all.
Besides that I really put in ALOT of time into this game, with excessive writing, 'cause I want to feel like I am a nation’s leader in heavy diplomacy. However, that excessive writing and agonising crunching over the game board on all options, costs tons of time. Time which is likely well spent when you are winning, but it feels less rewarding when you conjured up nice plan in several hours, only to be betrayed by your fellow players. Basically you fall into a downward spiral of: much time spent => orders do not deliver as promised => less time spent => less quality orders => more orders do not deliver => less time spent => less quality orders => etc. etc.
Also: because this game is very unforgiving it is important that you make sure everybody understands all the rules. Once a mistake is made - especially in the opening rounds (first year) - it is hard if not impossible to recover.
I will play Diplomacy again, but only face-to-face or in one weekend, PBEM is not my cup of tea.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
There's so little detail in the military aspect of the game that really it could have been anything else without anything being lost; rather than armies and fleets taking over supply centres to make more armies and fleets it could have been investors putting money in businesses to make more money. There's no morale, no terrain (so for example defending in Italy isn't any easier than attacking in Russia), no quality (so for example a Russian army will fight as well as a German one even though the actual warfare of the time shows very much otherwise), no supplies (so an army half the continent away will fight as well as if it was in its country's capital). Clearly the board was designed to make numbers and therefore alliances the key to winning even though in real life leadership, training and better weapons make the difference. When the board could as easily have been anything else there isn't much point to there being physical locations at all; it could have just been a series of counters and tracks and instead of moving pieces around you just negotiate for this and that which is really all the game is anyway.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1.5
I respect the design of the game, but I just get no enjoyment out of playing this.  I liken the game to 7 people driving bumperboats in the dark.  You simply must work with someone else for anything to be remotely effective otherwise you bump around blindly until someone does something stupid or the results of the chaos fall in your favor. Generally, when you decide to work with people you end up relying entirely on other people's lies.

So much time is wasted over a couple simple movements.  More often than not you just end up wasting your turns getting bumped.  This game feels like moving one step forward and one step back. More often than not you end up bumped or defending yourself to hold which is more or less the same as losing your turn.  There are very few games that I even remotely enjoy that have the ingenious "Lose your turn" aspect.

My experience with Diplomacy was an excercise in balancing irritation and boredom.  I normally love negotiation games, but this game is a joyless soul eater.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Is there another game out there which is this well-loved despite significantly unbalanced starting power levels?  I think Diplomacy gets off easy on the strength of its core movement/combat system (which is incredibly well-designed) and the fact that it's quite unique.  But the reality is that the "diplomatic" aspect of the game usually lasts for about 3-5 turns at the beginning, then crops up again when the 3-4 players at the end re-draw alliances based on the map.  Given all that, it's just not worth the time commitment to play this game FTF (not to mention the difficulties associated with plotting with your friends/enemies--everyone rushing off to a different room in pairs and feeling obliged to talk to **EVERYONE** else so as not to give anything away).

As a PBEM game, though...It's markedly better.  Perhaps the single best PBEM game I'm aware of.  The game is practically designed for it.

RATING: 9
Player interaction is at the heart of Diplomacy. The game mechanics are simple, even dull and boring, but the board and pieces are secondary in Diplomacy, will you stay true to your word, wil you betray an ally at an opportune moment, will they betray you? This intrigue lies at the heart of Diplomacy, a game infamous for ending friendships. It can be very difficult to gather a group, willing, able and mature enough to play, but when a game can be formed, all players will be rewarded with a unique experience that should not be passed up!

RATING: 9

It is the only game that gets me genuinely worried that the game may end abruptly and only for me.  I love that this game has pretty much zero luck except for maybe your country draw at the start.

Also, be wary of playing time.  The hardest part for me in this game is convincing people to finish.  Since it does not end at the same time for everybody that is another thing to consider. RATING: 2.1
Pointless if played quickly, tedious if played properly. In terms of raw game quality Diplomacy probably deserves a better ranking than this. But at this end of the scale the real criterion is "Which game is a less colossal waste of my time", and Diplomacy is a very long game.

[b]Possibility Space Landscape (Exploration=Fun)[/b]  [See my profile for more details.]
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Challenge (Depth) Fun derived from analyzing and predicting the optimal path to take through the possibility space landscape.
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Variety (Width) Fun derived from trying out a large variety of meaningful paths through the possibility space landscape.
:star::star::star::star::star: Atmosphere (Height) Fun derived from the experience or anticipation of encounters along a path through the possibility space landscape.

[b]Gaming Skills Employed[/b]
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Analysis
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Tactics
:star::star::star::star::star: Strategy
:star::star::star::star::star: Diplomacy
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Bluffing
:star::star::star::star::star: Player Reading
:nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Luck
:nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Knowledge
:nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Dexterity
:star::star::star::star::star: Gamesmanship

[b]Misc.[/b]
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Theme Integration
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Rules Complexity
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Bookkeeping
:nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Downtime
:nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Scalability

[b]Presentation[/b]
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Components
:star::nostar::nostar::nostar::nostar: Artwork
:star::star::star::star::star: Rules

[b]Comments[/b]
I play this mainly at http://www.dipbounced.com

Diplomacy is the ultimate game for testing your skills in manipulating and reading other players. It is not a very [i]Challenging game[/i], and its [i]Variety[/i] is limited by its fixed starting position and a lack of any random elements; however, it has the highest [i]Atmosphere[/i] of any game I have played. The skill of [i]Gamesmanship[/i] is an absolute requirement for this game. In order to derive fun from Diplomacy, you must embrace and personify the concepts of betrayal, lying, and manipulation, but you must recognize the boundaries between the possibility space of the game and reality.

RATING: 4

There are many rules for movement, and since it is simultaneous you can wind up with weird corner cases. That doesn't bother me though. In fact, it is actually a plus since it adds to the tactics of the game and makes you feel like a scheming war-strategist managing to pull of an unlikely maneuver.

Unfortunately it is way too backstabby to play with friends, which kind of ruins it for me. It's also player elimination, which sucks.

Nothing left to chance.  All strategy and negotiations!  The key is to know who to screw and when to screw them, as well as who to trust and when to trust them.

Best played with all 7 players.  (FYI - Austria was nicknamed "Dogmeat" in our games...)

A phenomenal game that brought hours and hours of excitement and fun.  

I own the vintage 1979 edition...

RATING: 7
Pros: influential area control game, strategic negotiations make up bulk of gameplay, extremely high level of player interaction, moderately tactical, no luck

Cons: player elimination, extremely lengthy playtime, limited player count, extremely prone to devastating kingmaking

I don't own the physical game and have only played face-to-face a few times and only ever with sub-optimal number of players. Obviously, this game is best with seven, and except perhaps for the Escalation variation (haven't tried that yet), I would only ever suggest it when seven players are available. However, if six of my friends and I ever had an afternoon completely free, I would petition HARD to get this to the table! I play  online regularly, and it is superb!

RATING: 7
I have played this couple of times as a teenager and two full games as an adult. First one was an email game second one used playdiplomacy.com as platform. One of the rare games which I prefer to play without sitting around the table, because it is good to have secret negotiations as secret and it is easier to stay within the time limits.

A classic game, simultaneous order phase followed by simultaneous action phase. It works quite nicely after you understand the basic of the rules (couple of rounds of the first game should be enough). The trick is that alone you can't accomplish that much, you need allies and you need to make some deals. And the backstab and get backstabbed. Not a game for everyone, I admit.

en mecatol
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
So simple, but human interaction facilities almost all the fun. And it IS fun. This game made me feel emotions I'm not sure I've ever even felt before... deciding when to betray someone, having a great offer come in from someone that you're not sure that you can trust, taking the offer, then instantly feel torn as you talk with your old ally. I've never mentally flip-flopped with my decisions in such a short period of time ever before. I was 100% dedicated to starting a huge war one min, then 100% against it the next due to some negotiations. 

From a immersion and break from reality/unique experience perspective, playing this with 5 other new players was unmatched. As a game, while I love the simplicity and understand why it's kept simple, it might be a little TOO simple for my own personal tastes. It also is a bit lacking in other game features, like requiring you to manually write out orders meticulously, or have a physical gaming space where you can get away from other players to scheme, or even being short enough to be able to actually finish a game in a session. The profound experience does counter-act most of these issues fairly well, though. My overall stance is that it's a brilliant, mechanically simple game akin to chess, but not a game I want to play constantly due to it's few but valid failings as a game.

After more plays, I can summarize this game as... frustrating. Either the fault is my own personality or the players I play with (Unlikely since I also play online with randoms and have similar experiences), but it seems many games come down to the same situation. I need to ally with the smaller of a 2 person alliance to backstab the dominant power who has overextended themselves. However, the smaller ally seems content with 2nd place and never does. I use logic. I appeal to their emotions. I appeal to friendship or metagame from older sessions... nothing works. 

This is a great game for the first 2-5 turns (and 2-5 hours) but it's like they forgot to design sensible end conditions and it just peters out into a frustrating mess or you get a victory because your ally was overly loyal. In summary, Diplomacy is a great system that desperately needs a redesign.

RATING: 9
Like Civilization, Diplomacy is a classic hampered a bit by its playing length and player-number constraints (the game really does demand a full complement of 7 players to be balanced and fun). Oddly enough, Diplomacy perhaps plays better over email or through the post than it does on a table, face-to-face; the most enjoyable games I've ever played were done this way, and the best stories I've heard of the game were played this way. Internet play also makes it quite possible to enjoy the game without having to roust up 7 players all by yourself, and this is the way I cut my teeth on the game, playing solidly on the internet for three years or so.

Diplomacy is an unremittingly nasty, interactive contest that counts as much on your negotiation skills as it does on your tactical/strategic skills. If you take nasty player interaction personally, or don't like it, do not play the game. I haven't played it in years, and probably would only play it at this point with people I knew, and who I knew would not take the game personally.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I don't really consider this my "gateway" because it didn't get me wondering what else was out there, but it was my introduction to hobby gaming. This is an absolute blast to play if you have the right group of people and a total nightmare with the wrong group. It's almost pure negotiation (though I think being able to see unconventional maneuvers and a few turns in advance is an under-appreciated key to victory), and it can get nasty. You really need to be able to leave things at the table. It also takes so long to play that I've never actually finished a game; usually after the second or third player elimination it's reasonably apparent who would win and we negotiate a ceasefire. The mechanics also make this a near-perfect play by e-mail game, and I've run several successful games this way. Not for everyone, but when it works, it's great.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
New version!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I'd refer to this game as the closest to multiplayer chess. On the plus side, there is no element of luck involved. You practically control your destiny with your own two hands. The rules are pretty straight forward and there is no downtime as each round is resolved simultaneously by all players involved. There is intense diplomatic bartering between players and this tends to appeal to all involved. On the negative side, you absolutely need 7 players present for the game to be properly played otherwise the ballance of the game is seriously ruined. Another big minus is that in the mid game inevitably some players are eliminated or fall so back that they have no chance to win the game and thus can loose interest. This I believe is the most serious defect of the game. Who wants to sit and observe others play while he sits back and does nothing for 2-3 hours?

RATING: 5
It's a classic, and a truly great design. But for me, three things stand out: (a) the illusion of control, (b) the ambiguity of the victory conditions (draws are...what, exactly?), and (c) the primacy of conspiracy over negotiation. The simultaneous resolution system is mostly clever (and would be entirely so were it not for stalemate lines), but it's too long and too random. The nastiness I rather enjoy, but early player elimination in such a long game is unforgivable. What kills this for me, though, is the fact that information you don't, and can't, know is what has the most import on your position.

RATING: 5
I really think this is a masterpiece but I don't think I will ever play it again that is why I don't give this a perfect 10.  People just get too mad at each other.  I am able to put my emotions to the side when I play this but no one else can. People just get too mad so I will not play this again. *correction - I am pretty good at putting my emotions to the side, but being repeatedly betrayed by all players does test that as I recently found out.

Played again recently, and I am downgrading this game to an 8 (from 9.5) (may drop lower if I think about this more). Elegant design. Just no fun for most the players involved. Try getting backstabbed, then backstabbed, then backstabbed, then backstabbed. 

That first turn is one of the best experiences in games, in general. But the next 7 turns are not fun if you didn't make any inroads.

Only a couple of the countries are any fun to play. 

This is probably the first time I have dropped a rating after losing (I don't know if I have ever really dropped a rating). Normally that doesn't bother me - losing that is. But losing in this game is not fun; especially since hope seems to be there each turn. So you are brought up and then taken down, then up, then down. That last game I played was wicked. And I have completely lost my love for this game. I won't play this again. All I know is I played my last game honourably.

Now I am just plain exhausted. All my previous feelings are gone, and I just am plan done with the game. Can 100% say I am done with the game. this game takes too long (played it online over several days) - don't want to play a board game for a long time now. (game is now a 5) Elegant design does not equal fun anymore.

RATING: 9.5
Played hundreds of times face-to-face and several times by e-mail or web. A game with multiple layers, most of which I really enjoy. The tactical rules are simple, yet allow for beautiful combinations; the negotiation multiplies the number of possibilities (even though I am not an expert at this part); and the tournament play adds even more subtleties. Reasonable people should understand that what happens in the game should stay in the game. I have met wonderful friends, visited lots of places, and had a lot of interesting experiences thanks to Diplomacy tournaments.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The classic game of negotiation and maneuver. Elegant mechanics keep the focus on the strategy and diplomacy. There's also a high incentive for aggressive moves, since a) "combat" usually doesn't lose you units, b) even peaceful borders can require a fair amount of bumping against each other, and c) small gains can translate into a significant strategic advantage. It's very cut-throat, though, and can be long compared to today's games. Online play solves this problem nicely.

Many say the game is about backstabbing, but primarily it's about building trust through moves on the board, cooperating to break down/fight off a common foe, and balance of power.
